<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41467"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The Spirodela polyrhiza genome reveals insights into its neotenous reduction fast growth and aquatic lifestyle"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2014 5:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2014-02-19"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2014 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="The subfamily of the Lemnoideae belongs to a different order than other monocotyledonous species that have been sequenced and comprises aquatic plants that grow rapidly on the water surface. Here we select Spirodela polyrhiza for whole-genome sequencing. We show that Spirodela has a genome with no signs of recent retrotranspositions but signatures of two ancient whole-genome duplications, possibly 95 million years ago (mya), older than those in Arabidopsis and rice. Its genome has only 19,623 predicted protein-coding genes, which is 28% less than the dicotyledonous Arabidopsis thaliana and 50% less than monocotyledonous rice. We propose that at least in part, the neotenous reduction of these aquatic plants is based on readjusted copy numbers of promoters and repressors of the juvenile-to-adult transition. The Spirodela genome, along with its unique biology and physiology, will stimulate new insights into environmental adaptation, ecology, evolution and plant development, and will be instrumental for future bioenergy applications. Spirodela, or duckweed, is a basal monocotyledonous plant with both pharmaceutical and commercial value. Here, the authors sequence the genome of Spirodela polyrhiza, suggesting its genome has evolved by neotenous reduction and clonal propagation, and provide a platform for future comparative genomic studies in angiosperms.
                  
                    
                  
                "/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>The subfamily of the <i>Lemnoideae</i> belongs to a different order than other monocotyledonous species that have been sequenced and comprises aquatic plants that grow rapidly on the water surface. Here we select <i>Spirodela polyrhiza</i> for whole-genome sequencing. We show that Spirodela has a genome with no signs of recent retrotranspositions but signatures of two ancient whole-genome duplications, possibly 95 million years ago (mya), older than those in Arabidopsis and rice. Its genome has only 19,623 predicted protein-coding genes, which is 28% less than the dicotyledonous <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> and 50% less than monocotyledonous rice. We propose that at least in part, the neotenous reduction of these aquatic plants is based on readjusted copy numbers of promoters and repressors of the juvenile-to-adult transition. The Spirodela genome, along with its unique biology and physiology, will stimulate new insights into environmental adaptation, ecology, evolution and plant development, and will be instrumental for future bioenergy applications.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>L<i>emnoideae</i>, commonly known as duckweeds, are the smallest, fastest growing and morphologically simplest of flowering plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Wang, W. et al. DNA barcoding of the Lemnaceae, a family of aquatic monocots. BMC Plant Biol. 10, 205 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d59549e912">1</a></sup>. The plant body is organized as a thalloid or ‘frond’ lacking a stem and in more derived species even roots (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>). Based on fossil records, the peculiar plant body architecture of this subfamily evolved by neotenous reduction from an <i>Araceae</i> ancestor and it has been interpreted botanically as juvenile or embryonic tissue<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Bogner, J. The free-floating Aroids (Araceae)-living and fossil. Zitteliana 48, 113–128 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d59549e922">2</a></sup>. The reduction and simplification of the plant body progresses within the <i>Lemnoideae</i> from ancient species like Spirodela towards more derived species like Wolffia. Although reduced flowers are observed in duckweeds, they usually reproduce by vegetative daughter fronds initiated from the mother frond (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>). Doubling time of the fastest growing duckweeds under optimal growth conditions is &lt;30 h, nearly twice as fast as other ‘fast-growing’ flowering plants and more than double that of conventional crops (more under <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Note 1</a>). They are easy to grow and have negligible lignin and high energy content in the form of easily fermentable starch (40–70% of biomass). Duckweeds have been used for the removal of high levels of contaminants from wastewater<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Cheng, J. J. &amp; Stomp, A. M. Growing duckweed to recover nutrients from wastewaters and for production of fuel ethanol and animal feed. CLEAN Soil Air Water 37, 17–26 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d59549e936">3</a></sup> and for the production of recombinant proteins for pharmaceutical applications<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Li, J. et al. Callus induction and regeneration in Spirodela and Lemna. Plant Cell Rep. 22, 457–464 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d59549e940">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Stomp, A. M. The duckweeds: a valuable plant for biomanufacturing. Biotechnol. Annu. Rev. 11, 69–99 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d59549e943">5</a></sup> and high-impact biofuel feedstock that does not compete for land in food production<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Hillman, W. The Lemnaceae, or duckweeds. Bot. Rev. 27, 221–287 (1961)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d59549e947">6</a></sup>. From a taxonomic point of view, genomic efforts have largely focused on the taxa of the <i>Commelinid</i> monocots such as the grasses from <i>Poales</i> and <i>Musa acuminata</i>, the wild-type diploid progenitor of banana, from <i>Zingiberales</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Systematics and biology of the Lemnoideae."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: <b>Systematics and biology of the</b> <b><i>Lemnoideae</i></b>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4311/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4311/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4311_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4311/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4311_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="657" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Duckweeds belong to the order <i>Alismatales</i> and the <i>Araceae</i> family, an early branch-off from the monocotyledonous crown ancestor. In agreement with previous classifications<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Bremer, K. Early cretaceous lineages of monocot flowering plants. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 97, 4707–4711 (2000)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d59549e991">70</a></sup>, (<b>a</b>) shows a phylogenetic tree of plastid-rbcL (ribulose-1, 5-bisphosphate carboxylase large-subunit) genes of two dicots—<i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> (<i>Brassicales</i>, NC_000932.1) and tomato (<i>Solanales</i>, NC_007898.2); three monocots <i>Spirodela polyrhiza</i> (<i>Alismatales</i>, NC_015891.1), rice (<i>Poales</i>, NC_001320.1) and banana (<i>Zingiberales</i>, EU017045.1), and water-lily as an outgroup (<i>Nuphar advena</i>, <i>Nymphaeales</i>, NC_008788). (<b>b</b>) shows a ventral view of Spirodela, illustrating schematically the clonal, vegetative propagation of duckweeds (redrawn and simplified from Landolt<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Landolt, E. The family of Lemnaceae—a Monographic Study Vol. 1Veroffentlichungen des Geobotanischen Institutes der Eidgenossischen Technischen Hochschule, Stiftung Rubel (1986)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d59549e1030">26</a></sup>). Daughter fronds (F1) originate from the vegetative node (No), from the mother frond F0 and remain attached to it by the stipule (Sti), which eventually breaks off, thereby releasing a new plant cluster. Daughter fronds may already initiate new fronds (F2) themselves before full maturity. Roots are attached at the prophyllum (P). (<b>c</b>) illustrates the progressive reduction from a leaf-like body with several veins and unbranched roots to a thallus-like morphology in the <i>Lemnoideae</i>, redrawn after historical illustrations ‘Das Pflanzenreich’ from <a href="http://www.biolib.de">www.biolib.de</a>; Sp: <i>Spirodela polyrhiza</i>, Le: <i>Lemna minor</i>, Wo: <i>Wolffia arrhiza</i>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4311/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Here we describe the genome and transcriptome of Greater Duckweed, <i>Spirodela polyrhiza</i>, representing the smallest monocot genome to date with a size of 158 Mb, which is similar to the plant model genome of <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>. Spirodela represents a basal monocotyledonous species from the <i>Alismatales</i> and will be an invaluable genomic resource to study the history of the monocotyledonous lineage.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Sequence assembly</h3><p>Genome sizes in the five <i>Lemnoideae</i> genera span an order of magnitude from 158 Mb in <i>Spirodela polyrhiza</i> to 1,881 Mb in <i>Wolffia arrhiza</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Wang, W., Kerstetter, R. A. &amp; Michael, T. P. Evolution of genome size in duckweeds (Lemnaceae). J. Bot. 2011, 1–9 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d59549e1097">7</a></sup>. Owing to its small size and basal position in the <i>Lemnoideae</i> we sequenced the <i>Spirodela polyrhiza</i> strain 7498 by whole-genome shotgun sequencing using ~20 × single end, ~1 × pair-end Roche/454 next-generation sequencing and ~1 × pair-end Sanger sequencing as described under Methods (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Table 1</a>). Although next-generation sequencing has been used to reduce the cost of sequencing genomes, short-read technologies have been insufficient to assemble chromosome-size molecules with megabase (Mb) genomes, especially for Spirodela, which does not have synteny with other fully sequenced genomes. Therefore, the read-length threshold of Roche/454 with a depth of 21 × in combination with long paired-reads from BACs and fosmids appeared to be a significant improvement in the balance of cost and genome sequence quality for new evolutionary references. Of the 158-Mb genome, as measured by flow cytometry (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>), 90% was assembled into contigs, 97% of the contigs assembled in 252 scaffolds and 94.1% of them in the top 50 largest scaffolds (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Table 2</a>).</p><p>To align the scaffolds with chromosomes, we constructed a Spirodela genomic library of bacterial artificial chromosomes (BACs) that was subjected to DNA fingerprinting, resulting in a physical map (Methods). BAC end sequences (BES) were used to align the assembled sequences with the physical map, providing us with proof of an accurate assembly of DNA sequences. We also used BACs that were aligned to the assembly with their sequenced ends to derive their entire sequence from the assembled sequences and used this information to select those that were low in repeat sequences for fluorescence <i>in situ</i> hybridization (FISH). Scaffolds were joined into 32 pseudomolecules, using the DNA fingerprinted physical map with anchored sequenced tagged sites (BESs), and one pseudomolecule labelled ‘0’ with all unanchored scaffolds (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>); (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Table 3</a>). Gaps in sequences like centromeres amounted to 10.7% of the genome and remained in unnamed bases (Ns).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Characteristics of the Spirodela genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Characteristics of the Spirodela genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4311/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4311/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4311_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4311/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4311_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="685" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>The outer circle shows the 32 pseudomolecules of the Spirodela genome assembly, tick scaling is 500 kb and blue and red bars depict position of telomeric and centromeric clusters. Heatmap tracks illustrate from outer to inner circle GC content, gene, repeat and GAGA-repeat densities. Colour map ranges are 30–50%, 0–30%, 0–70% and 0–1.5%, respectively. GC and gene content are positively, repeat and gene densities negatively correlated, whereas GAGA-repeats are present both in gene- and repeat-rich regions. The genome contains two rounds of ancient genome duplications. For each genomic segment, the copy number of paralogous regions is shown as bar chart in the innermost circle, duplication history is illustrated by red ribbons.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4311/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To examine how the 32 pseudomolecules relate to the 20 chromosomes of the haploid genome of <i>Spirodela polyrhiza</i>, we applied a cytogenetic analysis as described under Methods. FISH data supported the coherence of the 32 BAC-based pseudomolecules in distinct chromosome pairs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#Fig3">Fig. 3</a>). In two cases (pseudo #7 and #21) individual BACs were located on chromosomes other than the remaining contig. For instance, 002B12 and 035P14 labelled another chromosome than the other three BACs of pseudo #7. Thus, these two pseudomolecules were chimeric and each of the two arms had to be separated and joined to another arm to form one of the 20 chromosomes. Chimerism of pseudomolecules could be due to the short reads of the ‘454’ sequencing–platform and repeat-dense regions of the chromosomes. Although future work will have to convert pseudomolecules into chromosomes, the current analysis of the gene content and order remained unaffected.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Cytogenetics by FISH."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Cytogenetics by FISH.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4311/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4311/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4311_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4311/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4311_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="741" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Metaphase spread indicating a chromosome number of 2<i>n</i>=40 for <i>Spirodela polyrhiza</i> 7498. (<b>b</b>) Validation of BAC positions on a single chromosome pair by anchoring pseudo#6 of <i>Spirodela polyrhiza</i> via multicolour FISH. (<b>c</b>) The chimeric pseudo#7 was revealed by reprobing BACs with different fluorescence color schemes. Scale bars, 10 um.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4311/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To confirm that no other chimerism underlies the overall assembly quality and completeness, we localized telomeric repeats in the pseudomolecules. In higher plants, telomeres are characterized by tandem repeats of the conserved heptamer sequence TTTAGGG. Clusters of telomeric repeats were exclusively identified at the ends of the pseudomolecules, supporting the assumption that there were no hybrids of chromosomal arms (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>). Confirmation of the accuracy of sequence assembly was also possible with the distribution of repeat elements in the pseudomolecules described further below.</p><p>As an additional quantitative assessment of the completeness of the sequence assembly the Spirodela pseudomolecules were scanned for their content of ESTs, BES and 454 reads via masking (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Fig. 4</a>, assembly checker method). Three different-sized batches of randomly sampled 454 reads with 1, 2 and 5 × genome coverage, corresponding to Lander-Waterman statistics of 63, 87 and 99%, served as calibration sets with known genomic coverage. The Spirodela assembly contained 80% of the 1 × read test set and 90% of the EST and BES test sets. The content values for ESTs and BES were almost identical to the 5 × read set, which should represent the whole sequence amount. Overall the assembly completeness could be verified with the described new masking method to be at least 90% for genic sequences and ≥80% for the rest, which is in the same range as the values given in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Tables 4 and 5</a> (95.7% for ESTs, 83% for AraCyc genes).</p><p>To further ensure the quality of sequence assembly, we randomly selected 24 fosmids for conventional sequencing that were then aligned with the assembled 454 sequences and found that the sequencing error rates were 8 in 10,000, providing 98.22% accuracy (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Table 6</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Repeat elements</h3><p>The major sources of repeat elements in the genome are transposons and variable number tandem repeats (VNTRs). Other sources like high copy number genes (for example, rRNA genes) and different degrees of duplications (polyploidy, segmental duplications and tandem genes) usually contribute only to far lesser extent to total repeat sequences. To identify common and special features of the Spirodela genome the repeat data were put into a comparative context with the similar-sized <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> (At) (tigr 8 version)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Lamesch, P. et al. The Arabidopsis Information Resource (TAIR): improved gene annotation and new tools. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, D1202–D1210 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d59549e1229">8</a></sup> and three to four monocot genomes of different sizes, <i>Brachpodium distachyon</i> (Bd)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="The_International_Brachypodium_Initiative. Genome sequencing and analysis of the model grass Brachypodium distachyon. Nature 463, 763–768 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d59549e1236">9</a></sup>, <i>Oryza sativa</i> (rice) (Os)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="International_Rice_Genome_Sequencing_Project. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature 436, 793–800 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d59549e1244">10</a></sup>, <i>Sorghum bicolour</i> (Sb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature 457, 551–556 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d59549e1251">11</a></sup> and <i>Zea mays</i> (maize) (Zm)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Schnable, P. S. et al. The B73 maize genome: complexity, diversity, and dynamics. Science 326, 1112–1115 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d59549e1258">12</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Fig. 5</a>). Comparing the 16mer frequency of Spirodela with other plant genomes shows that the kmer curve of Spirodela follows a similar trend as the equally sized Arabidopsis genome. In both genomes kmers occurring ≥10 times are only found in ~3–4% of the sequence. In the larger monocot genomes, there is a continuous rise towards increase of genome size with kmers repeated ≥10 times starting from 12% in Brachypodium up to 63% in sorghum.</p><p>A homology search with <i>de novo</i> full-length long terminal repeat (LTR)-retrotransposons traces 13% of the Spirodela genome as LTR-retrotransposon-derived, which is perfectly in line with its small genome size (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#Fig4">Fig. 4</a>). A surprising result was the ratio of gypsy/copia LTR-retrotransposons of 3.5 in Spirodela, next to sorghum the second highest among the five genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#Tab1">Table 1</a>). An annotation attempt with RepeatMasker against mips-REdat_v9.3 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Nussbaumer, T. et al. MIPS PlantsDB: a database framework for comparative plant genome research. Nucleic Acids Res. 41, D1144–D1151 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d59549e1276">13</a>) gave an additional 2.5% of retroelements and 0.23% of DNA transposons. A closer inspection revealed that the DNA transposon hits were only based on small stretches of simple sequence repeats, which occurred within the template transposon sequence. The same and related problems were true for the additional retroelement hits from other species. Owing to their largely unspecific nature all transposon hits from non-Spirodela template sequences were removed from the final annotation. The observed lack of transposon similarity confirmed the large evolutionary distance between Spirodela and sequenced monocot genomes.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Linear dependency between genome size and LTR retrotransposon content for small-sized (&lt;&lt;1 Gb) plant genomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: Linear dependency between genome size and LTR retrotransposon content for small-sized (&lt;&lt;1 Gb) plant genomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4311/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4311/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4311_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4311/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4311_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="496" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Percentage of repetitive DNA is plotted against the genome size of different plant genomes, At for Arabidopsis, Sp for Spirodela, Bd for Brachypodium, Os for rice and Sb for sorghum.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4311/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Repeat composition of Spirodela compared with other plant genomes.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ncomms4311/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The percentage of tandem repeats (VTNRs) found in genome assemblies is relatively independent of genome size and ranged usually between ~2 to 3% (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#Tab1">Table 1</a>). The higher satellite repeat content of sorghum can be explained by its fully sequenced centromeres for three chromosomes. Spirodela has an exceptionally high proportion of microsatellite tandem repeats, 50% versus 3 to 6% in four reference genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#Fig5">Fig. 5</a>). This amplification even influences the absolute amounts of microsatellite repeats, as Spirodela tops the list with 1 Mb followed by the much larger sorghum genome with 0.9 Mb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#Fig5">Fig. 5b</a>). A detailed breakdown of microsatellite repeats into the different monomer sizes shows that one of the four possible dinucleotide repeats, namely ‘GAGA’, is responsible for the noticeable increased numbers of microsatellite repeats (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Fig. 6</a>). Owing to their high repetition, they severely impeded elongation during sequence assembly and were prevalently found at one or both ends of a pseudomolecule together with very high 16mer frequencies, especially often in the unplaced contigs of pseudo #0.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Composition of tandem repeat."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 5: Composition of tandem repeat.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4311/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4311/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4311_Fig5_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4311/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4311_Fig5_HTML.jpg" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="809" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) The percentage of tandem repeats in different plant genomes were broken down into satellite (yellow), minisatellite (blue), microsatellite (red) and composite satellite (grey) sequences. (<b>b</b>) The same comparison was made with respect to the absolute amount in Mb. Genomes were ordered according to size, with the lowest at the bottom, At for Arabidopsis, Sp for Spirodela, Bd for Brachypodium, Os for rice and Sb for sorghum.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4311/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Although Spirodela harbours almost the same amount of full-length LTR retrotransposons as the similar-sized Arabidopsis genome, the insertions are distinctly older (average 4.6 versus 2.0 mya) and very young elements have not been found. The age pattern of LTR-retrotransposon insertions in Spirodela shows distinct differences to other genomes, even to Arabidopsis. The Spirodela insertions are spread out over a longer time period, leading to higher average and median age values. The complete absence of very young full-length LTR-retrotransposons should be treated with caution, since it could also be an artefact of the ‘454’ sequencing platform, where identical sequence stretches tend to collapse. Still, the atypical age distribution suggests an ‘ancient’ genome state without much recent transposon activity in combination with small removal rates. The common picture in plant genomes of younger copia and older gypsy LTR-retrotransposons is weakly visible in Spirodela (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Fig. 7</a>).</p><p>The small genome size and atypical LTR age distribution of Spirodela suggested a tight control of transposon activity during recent evolutionary times. Both features might well be connected to the continuous clonal propagation of Spirodela. Transposon transcription is usually activated during seed development<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Slotkin, R. K. et al. Epigenetic reprogramming and small RNA silencing of transposable elements in pollen. Cell 136, 461–472 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d59549e2010">14</a></sup> and full-length LTR-retrotransposon elements are often removed by meiotic unequal crossing over between solo LTRs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Cai, X. &amp; Xu, S. S. Meiosis-driven genome variation in plants. Curr. Genomics 8, 151–161 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d59549e2014">15</a></sup>, two processes that are limited by the propensity of Spirodela to an asexual lifestyle. Also, in small genomes undergoing genome size reduction, transposition potentially could negatively impact gene activity, possibly requiring tighter regulation.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Gene number and transcriptome</h3><p>After the determination of repeat elements in the Spirodela genome these sequences could be filtered out to make the prediction pipeline for the gene content and their order in the 32 pseudomolecules more specific. We could identify 19,623 protein-coding genes in Spirodela (Methods), 28% less than Arabidopsis (27,416) and 50% less than rice (39,049) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#Tab2">Table 2</a>). Sources of other genomes and normalization of data sets are described under <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Discussion</a>. The Spirodela gene models were supported by 379,502 EST sequences assembled from transcriptome libraries generated from various diurnal time course and stress conditions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Table 7</a>). An unusual aspect of the gene content in the Spirodela genome is the local variability in GC composition, which exceeds variability observed in other plant genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#Fig6">Fig. 6</a>). Also, the differences between exon and intron GC content vary significantly between genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Fig. 8</a>). Still, the new <i>ab initio</i> algorithm was able to predict in a single run 19,327 genes, the number close to the final number of genes in annotation. The accuracy of the new algorithm was shown to be sufficiently high by assessment on a test set generated from mapping the transcripts and high-quality proteins (Sn/Sp of exact prediction of internal exons: 87.2%/74.5%). Mean exon and coding sequence sizes were similar in all five genomes. However, Spirodela shared with banana significantly larger gene sizes, which apparently resulted from longer introns. Composition heterogeneity of genomic sequence can be measured by s.d. of GC in fragments sampled along the genome. At fragment length 2,000 nt, the s.d. of GC content in <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> is five times higher (5 × ) than in a random sequence, whereas in <i>Spirodela polyrhiza</i> it was observed to be 9 × , thus evident of much higher composition heterogeneity than in <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> and even higher than in rice (8 × ), which is notoriously difficult for the analysis of statistical patterns related to protein-coding regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Fig. 9</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 Gene characteristics. This table shows the statistics of gene features for three monocotyledonous species (spirodela, rice and banana) and two dicotylodonous species (tomato and arabidopsis).</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ncomms4311/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Comparison of genome compositional heterogeneity in several plants."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 6: Comparison of genome compositional heterogeneity in several plants.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4311/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4311/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4311_Fig6_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4311/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4311_Fig6_HTML.jpg" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="574" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p>Standard deviation (s.d.) of GC content within a sequence fragment (window) is shown as a function of the window size for several plants and a random nucleotide sequence. The level of composition heterogeneity of genomic sequence is measured by s.d. of GC content defined in fragments of fixed length sampled along the genome.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4311/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Still, several lines of evidence supported the considerably lower gene number in Spirodela compared with other higher plant species. First, three independent gene prediction pipelines—the one described above, a self-training version of genemark and an approach using homology and transcriptome data—consistently predicted a gene number below 20,000 genes. Second, we observed a similar coverage of the AraCyc pathway genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Mueller, L. A., Zhang, P. &amp; Rhee, S. Y. AraCyc: a biochemical pathway database for Arabidopsis. Plant Physiol. 132, 453–460 (2003)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d59549e2552">16</a></sup> between monocotyledonous species: 87, 86 and 83% of the AraCyc genes were represented by a homologue (≥40% sequence identity, ≥70% alignment coverage) in the annotations of rice, banana and Spirodela, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Table 5</a>).</p><p>In comparison to dicots, increased GC contents were observed in monocotyledonous coding sequences, mainly due to a mutational bias of G and C in the third codon position. Spirodela protein-coding sequences exhibited a pronounced GC3 bias (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Fig. 9</a>), which was the highest amongst currently sequenced monocot genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#Fig6">Fig. 6</a>). Elevated GC3 contents were found in Spirodela specific genes as well as genes shared with monocots and dicots and thus seemed to be a general feature of Spirodela-coding sequences (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Fig. 10</a>). In contrast to the reported distinct bimodal distributions in rice and maize, Arabidopsis genes showed a sharp unimodal distribution. Broader distributions resulting from a composite of genes with low and high GC3 content were observed both for banana and Spirodela genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Fig. 9</a>), suggesting that the distinct multimodal patterns evolved specifically, whereas high GC3 biases might have evolved independently and several times in the monocotyledonous lineage.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Gene clusters and miRNAs</h3><p>Spirodela appeared to have a significantly lower number of tandem gene clusters (948) than rice (2,602), tomato (2,340) and Arabidopsis (1,938); however, surprisingly close to banana (1,048), which had ~1.9 times the gene number of Spirodela (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Table 8</a>). Interestingly, genome sequences of the latter two genomes are largely based on assemblies of next-generation sequencing technologies and some very closely related tandem genes may have collapsed in the assembly of short reads. However, considering the proportion of tandem genes relative to all genes, Spirodela does not deviate so much from other species (15.6% versus 19–21%), as banana does (7%). Therefore, the observed lower number of tandem genes can only partially compensate for the lower gene count in Spirodela.</p><p>A total of 413 miRNA loci comprising 93 families were identified in the Spirodela genome assembly by sequence similarity and structural features as described under Methods (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Table 9</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Gene distribution along chromosomes</h3><p>The annotation of both repeat elements and genes allows us to make a graphic representation of gene and repeat densities along the chromosomes (Methods). The heat map of the Spirodela pseudomolecules follows the known pattern of anti-correlation between gene and LTR-retrotransposon share of plant genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Fig. 11</a>). LTRs were largely absent from gene-rich regions but accumulate in gene-poor regions. There is a perfect correlation between prominent retrotransposon/kmer peaks and known centromeric locations in many other plant genomes (for example, sorghum, Brachypodium, rice, cotton, tomato and Arabidopsis). Here, based on high LTR-retrotransposon (&gt;80–90%) content together with high kmer values, the constrictions are to be seen as the most likely positions of the centromeres. The whole length of pseudomolecule #2 is detailed in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Fig. 12</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Organelle insertions</h3><p>A total of 1,385 chloroplast DNA fragment insertions covering 240,242 bp (0.15%) of the nuclear genome were identified. In all, 1,320 insertions detected were shorter than 500 bp, with 34 between 0.5 and 1 kb, 21 between 1 and 2 kb, and only 10 exceeding 2 kb with the largest being 5,197 bp (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Table 10</a>). A total of 1,589 mtDNA insertions into the nuclear genome covering 207,711 bp (0.13%) had been detected. Similar to the findings for the chloroplast insertions, 1,554 were &lt;500 bp in length, with 31 between 0.5 and 1 kb, 3 between 1 and 2 kb, and 1 exceeding 2 kb (2,185 bp) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Table 11</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Genome evolution</h3><p>We determined the syntenic relations between rice and Spirodela as described under Methods. The dot plot suggested a quota for the syntenic relation of 2:4 for rice and Spirodela duplicated segments, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Fig. 13</a>), indicating that the well-known ρ-WGD in grasses and the αSP/βSP-WGDs in Spirodela occurred independently of each other. The reported σ-duplication in grasses predating ρ had recently been placed after the split of the <i>Zingiberales</i> and <i>Poales</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="D'Hont, A. et al. The banana (Musa acuminata) genome and the evolution of monocotyledonous plants. Nature 488, 213–217 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d59549e2636">17</a></sup>. This study also reported an additional γ-WGD that was specific to the <i>Zingiberales</i> and hence had occurred after the split of the <i>Alismatales</i> and the core monocots. The <i>Alismatales</i>—together with the <i>Acorales—</i>represent the most ancient monocotyledonous clade that diverged from the core monocots, which included for example the <i>Commelinids</i>, <i>Asparagales</i> and <i>Liliales</i>, ~130 mya (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Janssen, T. &amp; Bremer, K. The age of major monocot groups inferred from 800+ rbcL sequences. Bot. J. Linn. Soc. 146, 385–398 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d59549e2661">18</a>). This placed the αSP/βSP-WGDs of Spirodela in the <i>Alismatales</i> branch (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#Fig7">Fig. 7</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-7" data-title="Phylogeny of monocotyledonous orders."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig7" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 7: Phylogeny of monocotyledonous orders.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4311/figures/7" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4311/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4311_Fig7_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-7-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4311/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4311_Fig7_HTML.jpg" alt="figure7" loading="lazy" width="685" height="502" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-7-desc"><p>The dendrogram is a simplified version redrawn from ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Janssen, T. &amp; Bremer, K. The age of major monocot groups inferred from 800+ rbcL sequences. Bot. J. Linn. Soc. 146, 385–398 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d59549e2683">18</a>. Core monocots are shown in brown, known WGDs are shown as blue circles.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4311/figures/7" data-track-dest="link:Figure7 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Copy numbers of duplicated chromosomal segments provided further support for two rounds of WGDs in Spirodela (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Fig. 14</a>). For about one third of the genome, no duplicated counterpart was observed. However, segments with copy numbers of four comprised approximately a quarter of the available genome sequence and were the second largest copy number class, followed by segments with three and two copies in the genome. Syntenic conservation between segmental blocks was significantly lower compared with those reported for grasses. Although syntenic regions between sorghum and rice contained on average 58% of the genes in collinear blocks<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature 457, 551–556 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d59549e2701">11</a></sup>, duplications in Spirodela showed a sparse conservation with a mean of 11.3% of syntenic paralogous pairs in collinear order (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Table 12</a>). This number might be an underestimate because global gene-based alignments between two blocks might miss small inversions or local translocations. Nevertheless, the reduced number was consistent with the older age of the presumed WGDs and a continuous loss of duplicated genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Zhang, L., Gaut, B. S. &amp; Vision, T. J. Gene duplication and evolution. Science 293, 1551 (2001)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d59549e2708">19</a></sup>. Synonymous substitution rates showed a unimodal distribution, indicating that both WGDs occurred within a short period of time (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Fig. 15</a>) and that they could not be separated by their divergence times. We therefore refer to the WGDs in Spirodela as αSP/βSP. There was a distinct shift in the mean peak <i>K</i>s values for GC3-high (mean ~0.85) and GC3-low (mean ~1.23) gene pairs. In this study and in agreement with other reports<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Tang, H., Bowers, J. E., Wang, X. &amp; Paterson, A. H. Angiosperm genome comparisons reveal early polyploidy in the monocot lineage. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 107, 472–477 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d59549e2719">20</a></sup>, we used the GC3-low paralogous pairs to estimate the occurrence of both WGDs at ~95 mya, which was older than the previously reported ρ-WGD in grasses and the γ-WGD in the Zingiberales.</p><p>Syntenic conservation of collinear gene pairs between rice and Spirodela was slightly higher than those of the αSP/βSP-WGDs, with a mean of 15% of co-orthologous pairs in the chromosomal segments showing conserved order. In total, the segments spanned 20,451 loci in rice and 11,479 in Spirodela, with 4,275 and 3,710 non-redundant collinear genes, respectively. Syntenic gene pairs between rice and Spirodela showed a pronounced bimodal distribution that was clearly caused by the superimposition of two unimodal distributions of GC3-high and GC3-low gene pairs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Fig. 16</a>). Following this rationale, we determined a mean peak <i>K</i>s of ~1.7 for the GC3-low distribution translating into a divergence time of ~130 mya. This estimate closely agrees with the divergence of the <i>Alismatales</i> and the core monocots that had been estimated to occur between 128–131 mya (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Janssen, T. &amp; Bremer, K. The age of major monocot groups inferred from 800+ rbcL sequences. Bot. J. Linn. Soc. 146, 385–398 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d59549e2734">18</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Gene families</h3><p>Gene families were selected based on prior knowledge about Spirodela biology and on biased representations of gene families, domains and biological processes, identified in our analysis of orthoMCL clusters as well as inter-species comparisons of selected gene families. An outline of our applied pipeline for genome-wide surveys of targeted gene families is shown in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Fig. 17</a>. Briefly, we compiled a list of gene identifiers either from publicly available curated gene families or by selection of genes with specific PFAM/InterPro domains from the AHRD annotations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="The_Tomato_Genome_Consortium. The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 485, 635–641 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d59549e2749">21</a></sup> (Methods).</p><p>The Spirodela genome contained very similar patterns of orthologous gene sets in comparison to four representative species (Arabidopsis, tomato, banana and rice), sharing a total of 8,255 common gene families despite a significantly reduced gene number (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#Fig8">Fig. 8</a>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Fig. 18</a>). However, Spirodela clusters generally showed the lowest average gene expansion and copy number, indicating preferred gene losses of duplicated genes in Spirodela or—<i>vice versa</i>—gene retentions in the other species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Table 13</a>). A notable exception from the overall conserved gene content was 750 orthoMCL clusters present in all four analysed species except Spirodela. These families included genes involved in water transport by aquaporins, phenylpropanoid, lignin biosynthesis and cell wall organization by expansins (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Table 14</a>). The loss of these gene families is consistent with the specialized morphology and lifestyle of Spirodela. Overrepresented functional categories of Spirodela-specific genes were enriched for various defence-related processes including antimicrobial peptides and adapted immune responses (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Table 15</a>) (further details on orthologous gene sets and on gene ontologies under <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Discussion</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-8" data-title="OrthoMCL analysis of gene families."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig8" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 8: OrthoMCL analysis of gene families.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4311/figures/8" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4311/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4311_Fig8_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-8-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4311/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4311_Fig8_HTML.jpg" alt="figure8" loading="lazy" width="685" height="520" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-8-desc"><p>The Venn diagram illustrates shared and distinct cluster classes from an orthoMCL analysis of the plant proteomes of <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i>, <i>Spirodela polyrhiza</i>, <i>Musa acuminata</i> and <i>Oryza sativa spp. indica</i>. Non-redundant data sets were used in the analysis. Numbers below each species name show number of all genes in species set and number of clustered genes, respectively. For each division in the Venn diagram, the top line shows the number of orthoMCL clusters and the bottom line the number of total genes in these clusters. The different cluster classes are colour-coded by the number of species containing genes for the respective class. Divisions for each class are shaded according to their abundance in their class with darker shades indicate larger contributions of the particular division.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4311/figures/8" data-track-dest="link:Figure8 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Morphogenesis and plant body architecture</h3><p>Expansins are cell-wall-loosening proteins<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Cosgrove, D. J. Loosening of plant cell walls by expansins. Nature 407, 321–326 (2000)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d59549e2821">22</a></sup> involved in many plant processes including cell growth and expansion, root and root hair expansion, fruit softening, ripening and abscission<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Choi, D., Lee, Y., Cho, H. T. &amp; Kende, H. Regulation of expansin gene expression affects growth and development in transgenic rice plants. Plant Cell 15, 1386–1398 (2003)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d59549e2825">23</a></sup>. We analysed α- and β-expansins by an integrative pipeline, which showed reduced copy numbers in Spirodela (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Figs 19 and 20</a>). Several clades of α-expansin genes were missing in Spirodela including AtEXP 2, 8, 17, 11, 7 and 18. The latter two expansins have been implicated in root hair initiation with AtEXP7 restoring a short root hair phenotype in rice, indicating orthologous functions of this expansin in monocotyledonous species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="ZhiMing, Y. et al. Root hair-specific expansins modulate root hair elongation in rice. Plant J. 66, 725–734 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d59549e2832">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Cho, H.-T. &amp; Cosgrove, D. J. Regulation of root hair initiation and expansin gene expression in Arabidopsis. Plant Cell 14, 3237–3253 (2002)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d59549e2835">25</a></sup>. Monocotyledonous plants have experienced a great expansion of β-expansins, with 10 detected in banana and on average 20 members in the <i>Poaceae</i> species. However, we detected only three β-expansins in Spirodela, indicating that the expansion continually progressed along the monocotyledonous diversification or a selective decrease of this gene family in Spirodela (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Fig. 20</a>) (further details on expansins under <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Discussion</a>).</p><p>Owing to the high buoyancy of their habitat, aquatic plants like duckweed do not require the vertical structural support like land plants that would be consistent with a reduction of genes involved in cell wall biosynthesis and lignification. Although cell wall biogenesis genes such as CesA, CslA, CslC and CslD were conserved across rice, Arabidopsis and Spirodela (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Table 16</a>), there were two unique rice clades of CslF and CslH and two unique Arabidopsis clades of CslB and CslG. Spirodela is missing comparable members of CslB, CslE, CslF, CslG and CslH (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Fig. 21</a>). We found all corresponding GT31 subfamily members in Spirodela, but the total copy number was 46.2% lower than in rice (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Fig. 22</a>). The missing five clades in the Csl family and the fewer members in the GT31 family are consistent with the low content of 4–16% cellulose in Spirodela<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Landolt, E. The family of Lemnaceae—a Monographic Study Vol. 1Veroffentlichungen des Geobotanischen Institutes der Eidgenossischen Technischen Hochschule, Stiftung Rubel (1986)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d59549e2861">26</a></sup> in comparison to 62% in rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Gani, A. &amp; Naruse, I. Effect of cellulose and lignin content on pyrolysis and combustion characteristics for several types of biomass. Renewable Energy 2007, 649–661 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d59549e2865">27</a></sup>, which might indicate that the contraction or lack of amplification of the cellulose biosynthesis gene family in Spirodela reflects its reduced requirement for rigid cell walls (further details on cellulose biosynthesis under <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Discussion</a>).</p><p>Lignin, a major component of secondary cell wall, plays an important role for support, water transport and stress responses in vascular plants. As shown in previous comprehensive phylogenetic analyses, most lignin biosynthesis gene families experienced rapid and recent duplications; the expansion mainly happened after the speciation between monocotyledonous and dicotyledonous species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Xu, Z. et al. Comparative genome analysis of lignin biosynthesis gene families across the plant kingdom. BMC Bioinformatics 10, (Suppl 11): S3 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d59549e2876">28</a></sup>. Although Spirodela contained nearly the entire lignin biosynthesis gene families with 9 out of 10 families (CAD, CCoAMT, 4CL, CCR, PAL, C4H, COMT, C3H, F5H but not HCT), gene copy number was significantly reduced compared with other monocotyledonous species like sorghum and rice (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Table 17</a>). This was consistent with previous genome analysis, where gene copy number constituted an important evolutionary force for specialization and traits. In addition to genes catalysing primary lignin biosynthesis, families involved in cell wall crosslinking and lignification also showed reduced copy numbers. We identified only seven members of the laccase multicopper enzymes in Spirodela, for which recent studies had provided experimental evidence for their role in lignification<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Berthet, S. et al. Disruption of LACCASE4 and 17 results in tissue-specific alterations to lignification of Arabidopsis thaliana stems. Plant Cell 23, 1124–1137 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d59549e2883">29</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Fig. 23</a>). This was consistent with previous analyses of 3.1% lignin in Spirodela<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Landolt, E. The family of Lemnaceae—a Monographic Study Vol. 2Veroffentlichungen des Geobotanischen Institutes der Eidgenossischen Technischen Hochschule, Stiftung Rubel (1987)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d59549e2890">30</a></sup> in comparison with 18% in rice straw<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Gani, A. &amp; Naruse, I. Effect of cellulose and lignin content on pyrolysis and combustion characteristics for several types of biomass. Renewable Energy 2007, 649–661 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d59549e2895">27</a></sup> (further details on lignin biosynthesis and laccases under <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Discussion</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Ecological adaptation</h3><p><i>Spirodela polyrhiza</i> can undergo an environmentally induced developmental switch from protein-rich vegetative leaf-like ‘fronds’ to a starch-rich dormant stage called ‘turion’<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Wang, W. &amp; Messing, J. Analysis of ADP-glucose pyrophosphorylase expression during turion formation induced by abscisic acid in Spirodela polyrhiza (greater duckweed). BMC Plant Biol. 12, 5 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d59549e2912">31</a></sup>. Unlike the linked vegetative fronds via stipule, turions fall from the mother fronds once mature after starch accumulation. They sink to the bottom of a pond and germinate into new fronds by using starch as energy. These functions require genes for starch biosynthesis including AGPase, SS plus GBSS, BE and DBE. Spirodela contained very similar gene family compositions as Arabidopsis (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Table 18</a>). The conservation of starch gene families from phylogenetic analysis for Spirodela, rice, maize and Arabidopsis argues for their essential functions. The clades of AGPase large subunit and DBE had multiple members, whereas all others contained only one single member for the corresponding subgroup. SpBEIII did not cluster with any clade, but provided a separate branch as a Spirodela-specific BE member, suggesting that it might have evolved into a special function from their common ancestor (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Fig. 24</a>) (further details on starch biosynthesis under <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Discussion</a>).</p><p>The high growth rates of Spirodela require the efficient usage of nutrients. Nitrogen is generally a major limiting factor of plant growth and a primary component in fertilizers to promote crop growth. However, leaching of fertilizers, increasing amounts of sewage and wastewater from a steadily growing world population results in water pollution. Spirodela has been successfully exploited for wastewater remediation because of its ability to remove nitrogen with high efficiency, particularly in the form of ammonia, from polluted water<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Stomp, A. M. The duckweeds: a valuable plant for biomanufacturing. Biotechnol. Annu. Rev. 11, 69–99 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d59549e2928">5</a></sup>. Glutamine synthetase (GS) and glutamate synthase (GOGAT) are the core enzymes of the GS/GOGAT cycle in plants, the major biochemical module for ammonium assimilation. Despite a genome-wide reduction in gene number, copy numbers of these enzymes were retained or even amplified in Spirodela with up to four times more copies of Fd-GOGAT in Spirodela compared with Arabidopsis and rice (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#Fig9">Fig. 9</a>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Fig. 25</a>) (further details on nitrogen efficiency under <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Discussion</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-9" data-title="Spirodela characteristic pathways."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig9" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 9: Spirodela characteristic pathways.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4311/figures/9" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4311/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4311_Fig9_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-9-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4311/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4311_Fig9_HTML.jpg" alt="figure9" loading="lazy" width="685" height="999" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-9-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) A schematic diagram of nitrogen assimilation in higher plants is illustrated. Spirodela shows a high overrepresentation of enzymes of the GS/GOGAT cycle, the major module for ammonia assimilation and consistent with the high ability of Spirodela to remove ammonia from sewage and wastewaters. Copy numbers for each gene are shown in red for Spirodela, blue for rice and green for Arabidopsis. (<b>b</b>) A highly simplified scheme of the regulatory network of the juvenile-to-adult phase transition in Arabidopsis is illustrated. Most genes have several, functionally similar paralogs and are only shown for simplicity reasons by one gene symbol.
