For example, APETALA1, CAULIFLOWER and FRUITFUL are close paralogs promoting the onset of an inflorescence meristem but are represented only by one gene symbol, AP1. Gene groups having either similar copy numbers or being overrepresented in Spirodela are shown in red; those that have significantly reduced numbers are shown in blue.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4311/figures/9" data-track-dest="link:Figure9 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Development and reproduction</h3><p>Flowering plants undergo a series of distinct phase transitions during their life cycle, including the progression from a vegetative or juvenile phase to an adult phase with competency for sexual reproduction (flowering). Neoteny, the prolongation of juvenile traits, is a common phenomenon in the evolution of plant organs. The frond of the <i>Lemnoideae</i> has been characterized as embryonic or juvenile tissue, or as a cotyledon-like plant, iteratively bearing new cotyledons (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>).</p><p>Although Spirodela has an increased copy number of repressors of the transition from juvenile to adult phase in comparison to Arabidopsis and rice, components of the regulatory network enhancing the progression through the adult phase and the onset of an inflorescence meristem were reduced (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#Fig9">Fig. 9</a>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Table 19</a>), for example, SPB (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Fig. 26</a>), MADS-box (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Fig. 27</a>) and PEBP gene families (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Fig. 28</a>). In Arabidopsis, the microRNA of miR156 is necessary and sufficient to promote the juvenile phase and inhibit the transition to the adult growth<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Wu, G. et al. The sequential action of miR156 and miR172 regulates developmental timing in Arabidopsis. Cell 138, 750–759 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d59549e3001">32</a></sup>. Copies of miR156 were highly abundant in Spirodela, with 24 loci, or up to 32 loci if highly similar isoforms were included, consistent with the pattern of preferentially retained repressors of the adult phase, whereas Arabidopsis had only 10 and rice had 19 loci. The opposite was true for miRNA169, involved in drought tolerance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Zhao, B. et al. Identification of drought-induced microRNAs in rice. Biochem. Biophys. Res. Commun. 354, 585–590 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d59549e3005">33</a></sup>, and miRNA172, involved in the switch from juvenile to adult phase<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Lauter, N., Kampani, A., Carlson, S., Goebel, M. &amp; Moose, S. P. microRNA172 down-regulates glossy15 to promote vegetative phase change in maize. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 102, 9412–9417 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d59549e3009">34</a></sup>, which were reduced from 9 and 5 copies found in Arabidopsis and tomato, respectively, to 1 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Table 9</a>) (further details on the transition from juvenile to adult phase under <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#MOESM676">Supplementary Discussion</a>).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec14" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec14-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec14">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec14-content"><p>In higher plants, gene number and genome size seem to be not correlated. Although <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> has a genome size similar to Spirodela, it contains ~28% more genes. The low gene count of Spirodela could in part be due to the structural reduction and juvenile nature reducing the need for and consequently the retention or duplication of genes acting in the adult phase. In addition, Spirodela differs from previously reported angiosperm genomes in its lack of recent WGDs and retrotranspositions. The lower gene number in Spirodela may therefore be simply a consequence of the ongoing non-functionalization and loss of one copy of a duplicated gene pair, a major fate of gene duplication<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Zhang, L., Gaut, B. S. &amp; Vision, T. J. Gene duplication and evolution. Science 293, 1551 (2001)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d59549e3031">19</a></sup>. We propose that the predominant vegetative reproduction and low flowering frequency as well as the reduced and simple plant body of Spirodela is at least in part a consequence of the re-engineering of the genetic network that controls transitions to the adult and flowering growth phases. Interestingly, structural reduction increases in the <i>Lemnoideae</i> from the more ancient species like Spirodela towards the more derived members such as Wolffia (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4311#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>). Genomes of duckweeds should therefore present an excellent opportunity to study how different degrees of neoteny translate into molecular changes of developmental networks and gene families. As Spirodela provides us with a unique and fascinating biology, its genome sequence will serve us for future evolutionary and comparative genomic studies among angiosperms. In addition to basic question in plant evolution and development, applications of duckweeds in water remediation and as a renewable energy source can now be further optimized. The genome sequence of Spirodela provides the first step to identify, understand and improve relevant traits for specific target applications.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec15" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec15-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec15">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec15-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Genomic DNA isolation</h3><p>One cluster of 3–5 fronds of <i>Spirodela polyrhiza</i> strain 7,498, which were clonally grown from one plant to reduce potential sequence polymorphism, was aseptically transplanted into half-strength Schenk and Hildebrandt basal salt mixture (Sigma, S6765) with 1% sucrose liquid medium at pH 5.8. The cultures were kept in a growth-chamber, maintained at 100 μmol m<sup>−2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>and 23 °C through a 16 h-light, 8 h-dark photoperiod<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Wang, W. &amp; Messing, J. Analysis of ADP-glucose pyrophosphorylase expression during turion formation induced by abscisic acid in Spirodela polyrhiza (greater duckweed). BMC Plant Biol. 12, 5 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d59549e3060">31</a></sup>. High molecular weight of nuclear DNA was extracted by adaption of a nuclei isolation procedure and the CTAB method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Wang, W. &amp; Messing, J. High-Throughput sequencing of three lemnoideae (duckweeds) chloroplast genomes from total DNA. PLoS One 6, e24670 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d59549e3064">35</a></sup>. Simply, after grinding 10 g of frozen tissue in liquid nitrogen, nuclei were isolated with a sucrose-based buffer, and then suspended in 50 ml CTAB extraction buffer. The isolated DNA was digested with 50 μg ml<sup>−1</sup> RNase for 1 hour at 37 °C. The quality and quantity were checked with a 1% gel and measured with Nanodrop 1000.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Haploid genome size estimation</h3><p>For flow cytometric genome size estimations 10 mg of fresh duckweed tissue were chopped together with similar amounts of an internal reference standard <i>Raphanus sativus</i> ‘Voran’ (IPK gene bank accession number RA 34, 543 Mb) with new razor blades in propidium iodide-containing nuclei isolation buffer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Dolezel, J., Greilhuber, J. &amp; Suda, J. Estimation of nuclear DNA content in plants using flow cytometry. Nat. Protoc. 2, 2233–2244 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d59549e3082">36</a></sup>. Measurements were performed on a FACStarPLUS cell sorter (BD Biosciences, San Jose, CA, USA). The calculated average value is based on at least 10 independent measurements performed on separate days.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Genome sequencing and sequence assembly</h3><p>A high-quality genome sequence was produced with the Roche/454 and Sanger ABI-3730XL platforms, using the whole-genome shotgun sequencing method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Gardner, R. C. et al. The complete nucleotide sequence of an infectious clone of cauliflower mosaic virus by M13mp7 shotgun sequencing. Nucleic Acids Res. 9, 2871–2888 (1981)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d59549e3094">37</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Messing, J., Crea, R. &amp; Seeburg, P. H. A system for shotgun DNA sequencing. Nucleic Acids Res. 9, 309–321 (1981)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d59549e3097">38</a></sup>. Sequencing reads for the nuclear genome were collected with the Roche 454 XLR next-generation sequencing platform at the Department of Energy Joint Genome Institute (JGI) in Walnut Creek, CA, USA, according to the manufacturer’s specification (454 Life Sciences, Branford, CT, USA). Two linear Roche 454 libraries (8 runs, 2.95 Gb) and one 5.7 kb insert size paired library (343.4 Mb) were sequenced with standard XLR protocols. Paired ends were also generated from BAC and fosmid libaries and 24 entire finished fosmids were obtained using standard protocols on ABI3730XL machines at the HudsonAlpha Institute in Huntsville, AL, USA, and the JGI according to the manufacturer’s specification (Life Technologies, Grand Island, NY, USA). The assembly was generated using Newbler version 2.6 with default parameters after trimming poor bases from ends and masking vector sequences.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Physical map and pseudomolecules</h3><p>A Spirodela BAC library was constructed from high-molecular weight nuclear DNA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Wang, W. &amp; Messing, J. High-Throughput sequencing of three lemnoideae (duckweeds) chloroplast genomes from total DNA. PLoS One 6, e24670 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d59549e3109">35</a></sup>. The DNA was partially digested with HindIII, double size-selected and ligated into pIndigoBAC-5 (HindIII-cloning Ready, Epicentre, BACH095H, Madison, WI, USA). A total of 15,360 Spirodela BAC clones with an average insert size of 110 kb representing 10 × genome equivalents were fingerprinted with the SNaPshot HICF fingerprinting method using the LIZ1200 size standard as described somewhere else<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Gu, Y. Q. et al. A BAC-based physical map of Brachypodium distachyon and its comparative analysis with rice and wheat. BMC Genomics 10, 496 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d59549e3113">39</a></sup>. Of the 15,360 fingerprinted clones, 11,770 clones (76.6%) were suitable for contig assembly. There was on average one restriction fragment every 1.19 kb. The final FPC map spanned 200 Mb and contained 269 singletons and 11,501 BAC clones, which were integrated into 320 contigs. In the physical map, 23 contigs had more than 100 clones each, 62 contigs had 50–99 clones each, 160 contigs had 10–49 clones and the residual 75 had &lt;10 clones. Based on the physical map integration, the Newbler scaffolds were ordered by the FPC draft sequence function and pseudomolecules were constructed from joined scaffolds. Scaffolds within one pseudomolecule were interlaced by a stretch of 500 undefined bases (‘N’s).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Cytogenetics</h3><p>To align the 32 BAC-based pseudomolecules with linkage groups represented by individual chromosome pairs, we applied FISH. BACs with a low repeat content according to RepeatMasker ( <a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/">http://www.repeatmasker.org/</a>) analysis were assembled into contigs spanning the physical map of <i>Spirodela polyrhiza</i>. These BAC contigs were subdivided into appropriate probes for FISH. Metaphase chromosome spreads of Spirodela were prepared from young root tips treated with 0.002 M 8-hydroxyquinoline for 1 h on ice before fixation in absolute ethanol: acetic acid (3:1, <i>v</i>/<i>v</i>) for 48 h and digestion for 90 min at 37 °C in 1% cellulase and 1% pectinase in 0.01 M sodium citrate at pH 4.8. Digested root tips were squashed in 75% acetic acid on slides and frozen on dry ice. BAC probe labelling, FISH, microscopic evaluation and image processing were performed as described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Schubert, V. et al. Sister chromatids are often incompletely aligned in meristematic and endopolyploid interphase nuclei of Arabidopsis thaliana. Genetics 172, 467–475 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d59549e3142">40</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Assembly check</h3><p>The so called ‘assembly checker’ is based on sequence content assessment by masking one sequence set with another via the programme vmatch ( <a href="http://www.vmatch.de">http://www.vmatch.de</a>). The approach introduces a versatile alternative method for the quantification of assembly completeness. Whole-genome sequence sets, like transcripts, reads and BESs are used as test sets to determine their percent base-pair coverage with the genome assembly. After the evaluation of different matching stringency, the parameters setting ‘−l 50 −e 1’ (=minimum hit length 50 bp, maximal 1 mismatch or indel per 50 bp) was found to be suitable for the Spirodela sequence sets. Here the matching of two different random sets of 1 × genome coverage against each other gave 63% coverage, which is exactly the Lander–Waterman expectation.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">RNA isolation and cDNA sequencing</h3><p>To collect a diverse set of expressed genes, <i>Spirodela polyrhiza</i> was grown under different light–dark cycles (24 h/0 h, 16 h/8 h, 12 h/12 h, 8 h/16 h and 0 h/24 h) and collected at time points that represent various states of the circadian clock (02:00, 06:00, 10:00, 14:00, 18:00 and 22:00). In addition, <i>Spirodela polyrhiza</i> cultures were treated by various stress conditions (heat treatment at 37 °C, cold treatment at 0 °C, desiccation on agar plate, high pH value of 9, UV exposure, 20 mg l<sup>−1</sup> CuCl<sub>2</sub>, 300 mg l<sup>−1</sup> KNO<sub>3</sub>, 250 nM ABA, 10 μM kinetin, 300 mM mannitol) and samples were collected at different exposure times (0.5 h, 1 h, 3 h, 6 h, 12 h and 24 h). Fresh tissue (0.2 g) was collected from each conditions and flash-frozen in liquid nitrogen. High-quality total RNA was extracted with the RNeasy Plant Mini Kit (Qiagen, 74904). The on-column DNase I was used to remove contaminating genomic DNA (Qiagen, 79254). We used gel electrophoresis and Nanodrop 1,000 to assess RNA quality and quantity. Finally, equal amounts of RNA were pooled from each sample.</p><p>The library was constructed for RNA samples and sequenced with the ‘454’ platform. ESTs were assembled using the Joint Genome Institute EST sequence-processing pipeline. Briefly, raw 454 EST sequences were trimmed for vector and adaptor/linker sequences and poor reads. Contaminants were also screened and filtered by BLAST alignment. ESTs were clustered using malign and assembled using CAP3 to build tentative consensus sequences.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">Repeat analysis</h3><p>Kmer frequencies are a repeat library independent and thus unbiased method to access the repetitive portion of a genome. The programme tallmyer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Kurtz, S., Narechania, A., Stein, J. C. &amp; Ware, D. A new method to compute K-mer frequencies and its application to annotate large repetitive plant genomes. BMC Genomics 9, 517 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d59549e3195">41</a></sup> from the programme suite genome tools ( <a href="http://genometools.org/">http://genometools.org/</a>) was used to calculate the frequency of each 16-mer in the respective genome assemblies and other sequence sets.</p><p>Complete LTR retrotransposons where identified in a <i>de novo</i> approach with the programme LTR-STRUC<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="McCarthy, E. M. &amp; McDonald, J. F. LTR_STRUC: a novel search and identification program for LTR retrotransposons. Bioinformatics 19, 362–367 (2003)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d59549e3212">42</a></sup>. Quality filtering was based on &lt;30% tandem repeat content, at least one typical inner protein domain, manual dot plot inspection and removal of overlapping sequences. Additional complete LTR-retrotransposons were detected by homology search against the full-length sequences. The insertion age of full-length LTR-retrotransposons was derived from the divergence (emboss distmat with Kimura 2 parameter distance) between the left and right LTR sequences, which were identical after transposition as described elsewhere<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="SanMiguel, P., Gaut, B. S., Tikhonov, A., Nakajima, Y. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. The paleontology of intergene retrotransposons of maize. Nat. Genet. 20, 43–45 (1998)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d59549e3216">43</a></sup>.</p><p>Transposons and rRNA genes were annotated by the wublast version of RepeatMasker-open-3-3-0 ( <a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org">http://www.repeatmasker.org</a>) against the mipsREdat (REdat_v9.3, 387 Mb, 56,169 entries). The RepeatMasker output was subjected to two post-processing filter steps, removal of low confidence hits (length &lt;50 bp or score &lt;250 or identity &lt;60%) and purification of overlapping annotations in a priority-based approach, where higher score hits were assigned first and overlapping lower score hits either shortened or, if the overlap exceeded 90% of their length, removed.</p><p>Tandem repeat sequences were detected with the programme Tandem Repeats Finder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Benson, G. Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 27, 573–580 (1999)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d59549e3233">44</a></sup> under default parameters. Classification of the tandem repeats were based on their monomer length and divided into microsatellites (2–9 bp), minisatellites (10–99) and satellites (≥100bp). Overlapping annotations were joined and classified as hybrid type, if they contained more than one of the three classes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec24">Gene prediction and annotation</h3><p>Gene models were derived from consensus gene predictions based on <i>de novo</i> gene finders, transcript data and protein homologies. EST assemblies of Spirodela and of two sea grasses, <i>Posidonia oceanica</i> and <i>Zostera marina</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Wissler, L. et al. Dr Zompo: an online data repository for Zostera marina and Posidonia oceanica ESTs. Database (Oxford) 2009, bap009 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d59549e3253">45</a></sup>, were used as transcript evidences. Heterologous protein evidence was based on protein sequences of four monocotyledonous species—Brachypodium, maize, sorghum and rice—and three dicotyledonous species, Arabidopsis, poplar and Vitis. For evidence by homology, spliced alignments were generated by GenomeThreader<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Gremme, G., Brendel, V., Sparks, M. E. &amp; Kurtz, S. Engineering a software tool for gene structure prediction in higher organisms. Inform. Software Technol. 47, 965–978 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d59549e3257">46</a></sup> using an initial seed size of seven amino acids for protein and 16 bp for nucleotide alignments. For <i>de novo</i> gene finders, a training set was derived from mapping the Spirodela EST assemblies and high-quality protein families to scaffold sequences. As high-quality proteins, we selected orthologous gene families from the PLAZA database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Proost, S. et al. PLAZA: a comparative genomics resource to study gene and genome evolution in plants. Plant Cell 21, 3718–3731 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d59549e3265">47</a></sup>, for which at least five distinct plant species had members differing by a maximum of 2% in the protein sizes from the mean family sequence size. Next, we performed multiple sequence alignments of the selected families applying MUSCLE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Edgar, R. C. MUSCLE: a multiple sequence alignment method with reduced time and space complexity. BMC Bioinformatics 5, 113 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d59549e3269">48</a></sup> to confirm sequence similarity and size consistency in the alignments. Spliced alignments to Spirodela genomic scaffolds were computed using GenomeThreader and filtered for full-length alignments including start and stop codons, high similarity (blosum62 score ≥ twice size of alignment) and size consistency with the respective gene family. Remaining gene models for training were selected to be non-redundant both in terms of individual, overlapping mappings of members of one family as well as mappings of one family to multiple genomic locations. To derive full-length Spirodela transcripts from the EST assemblies, candidate ORFs were predicted by applying ORFpredictor<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Min, X. J., Butler, G., Storms, R. &amp; Tsang, A. OrfPredictor: predicting protein-coding regions in EST-derived sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, W677–W680 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d59549e3273">49</a></sup> with pre-computed tblastx comparisons against a database compilation of Arabidopsis, Sorghum and Brachypodium proteins. Only ORFs with similarity to known proteins and full-length alignments to a genomic position of Spirodela scaffolds including start and stop codon were retained. Finally, we compiled a non-redundant training set as described above for the PLAZA proteins.</p><p>Using the non-redundant data sets described above, we trained four <i>de novo</i> gene prediction tools, Augustus, Snap, GlimmerHMM and GeneID<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Stanke, M. et al. AUGUSTUS: ab initio prediction of alternative transcripts. Nucleic Acids Res. 34, W435–W439 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d59549e3283">50</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Korf, I. Gene finding in novel genomes. BMC Bioinformatics 5, 59 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d59549e3286">51</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Majoros, W. H., Pertea, M. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. TigrScan and GlimmerHMM: two open source ab initio eukaryotic gene-finders. Bioinformatics 20, 2878–2879 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d59549e3289">52</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Guigó, R., Agarwal, P., Abril, J. F., Burset, M. &amp; Fickett, J. W. An assessment of gene prediction accuracy in large DNA sequences. Genome Res. 10, 1631–1642 (2000)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d59549e3292">53</a></sup> and determined genome-wide predictions using the Spirodela-specific parameter sets of each tool. An additional gene finder, Fgenesh+, was run using a monocotyledonous-specific parameter matrix<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Salamov, A. A. &amp; Solovyev, V. V. Ab initio gene finding in drosophila genomic DNA. Genome Res. 10, 516–522 (2000)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d59549e3296">54</a></sup>. Next, the statistical combiner Jigsaw was trained using our training set, mapped homologies and gene models predicted by our set of <i>de novo</i> gene finders<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Allen, J. E. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. JIGSAW: integration of multiple sources of evidence for gene prediction. Bioinformatics 21, 3596–3603 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d59549e3303">55</a></sup>. The resulting gene models constituted version 1.0.</p><p>For historical reasons, an independent set of predictions was made with a new self-training <i>ab initio</i> gene finder, GeneMark-ES-GC, developed for compositionally heterogeneous genomes (Lomsadze and Borodovsky, unpublished). The GeneMark-ES-GC gene predictions were later merged with the models produced by computational homologies and models the other <i>de novo</i> predictions including the Jigsaw models. Upon merging, <i>de novo</i> models with no significant homology (blastp <i>E</i>-value &gt;10–10 versus UniprotKB/Swissprot) were included, if at least two independent predictions supported an identical gene structure. Finally, models from the training set were integrated into this set of gene models to obtain the final set of consensus gene predictions, to which we refer as version 2.0.</p><p>Mature miRNA sequences of all plant species present in miRBase version 19 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Kozomara, A. &amp; Griffiths-Jones, S. miRBase: integrating microRNA annotation and deep-sequencing data. Nucleic Acids Res. 39, D152–D157 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d59549e3324">56</a>) were mapped to the Spirodela whole-genome assembly using vmatch<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Abouelhoda, M. I., Kurtz, S. &amp; Ohlebusch, E. The enhanced suffix array and its applications to genome analysis. Lect. Notes. Comput. Sci. 2452, 449–463 (2002)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d59549e3328">57</a></sup>, allowing up to two mismatches. Subsequently, 150-bp flanking sequences adjacent to the 5′- and 3′-boundaries of putative miRNAs were retrieved and their secondary structure was predicted using RNAfold<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Denman, R. B. Using RNAFOLD to predict the activity of small catalytic RNAs. BioTechniques 15, 1090–1095 (1993)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d59549e3332">58</a></sup> with standard settings. The structure was evaluated using MIRcheck with default settings<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Jones-Rhoades, M. W. &amp; Bartel, D. P. Computational identification of plant microRNAs and their targets, including a stress-induced miRNA. Mol. Cell 14, 787–799 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d59549e3336">59</a></sup>. Putative miRNAs passing MIRcheck were retained and overlapping loci of matched miRNAs were concatenated and annotated as one miRNA.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec25">Tandem genes</h3><p>An undirected graph was constructed from a self-comparison of each proteome using blastp with protein identifiers as nodes and edges, which specified similarity matched between two proteins and were weighted by expectation values. A first filter removed all matches above a threshold <i>E</i>-value <i>E</i>&gt;10–10. Next, only edges connecting two proteins with a genomic distance of &lt;10 dissimilar intervening genes were retained. Tandem clusters were determined as connected components from this trimmed graph.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec26">Organellar insertions</h3><p>The assembled nuclear genome of Spirodela was compared by blastn against the Spirodela plastid genome (JN160603)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Wang, W. &amp; Messing, J. High-Throughput sequencing of three lemnoideae (duckweeds) chloroplast genomes from total DNA. PLoS One 6, e24670 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d59549e3363">35</a></sup> and the mitochondrial genome (JQ804980)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Wang, W., Wu, Y. &amp; Messing, J. The mitochondrial genome of an aquatic plant, Spirodela polyrhiza. PLoS One 7, e46747 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d59549e3367">60</a></sup>, respectively, to identify the insertion of organellar sequences into the nuclear genome. We retained all hits longer than 50 bp and hits were categorized by their size.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec27">Heat maps</h3><p>Heat maps and stacked bar charts are used to visualize and compare specific chromosomal content from a bird’s eye perspective. The higher-level heat map data were created by sliding along the chromosome with a 0.1-Mb window size and 0.02-Mb shift length and determining for each window the number and percentage of bp coverage of the respective element type, like genes or LTR-retrotransposons. For kmer frequencies the mean and median values per window was used. The density values were corrected for the number of Ns per window, if the N content exceeded 60% the value was set to null and drawn in grey colour. The number value was extrapolated to number per Mb to facilitate comparisons. The heat maps were created from the obtained density values using the python pylab module in combination with the jet colour map (low to high values from blue to red).</p><p>A more detailed insight into the annotation structure was achieved with the integrative genome viewer ( <a href="http://www.broadinstitute.org/igv/">http://www.broadinstitute.org/igv/</a>) by defining customized tracks for the different element types and kmer values in combination with special colour codes and display options.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec28">Synteny</h3><p>Analysis of genome duplications were based on all-against-all blastp comparisons between non-redundant gene sets of the respective species (<i>E</i>-value cutoff <i>E</i>&lt;10–10). Intra- and intergenomic duplicated segments were identified by a combination of quota alignments<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Tang, H., Bowers, J. E., Wang, X. &amp; Paterson, A. H. Angiosperm genome comparisons reveal early polyploidy in the monocot lineage. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 107, 472–477 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d59549e3403">20</a></sup> (exploring various quota settings for the expected evolutionary history of genome duplications) and manual inspection and curation of dot plots. Genes between candidate duplications were aligned by a global alignment similar to the methods described elsewhere<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Haas, B. J., Delcher, A. L., Wortman, J. R. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. DAGchainer: a tool for mining segmental genome duplications and synteny. Bioinformatics 20, 3643–3646 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d59549e3407">61</a></sup>. Statistical significance of candidate duplications was evaluated by a Monte Carlo test. Briefly, the gene order in one genome was randomly shuffled 1,000 times by exchanging gene identifiers, and gene alignments were recomputed according to the initial genomic borders of candidate segments. Alignments of the random genomes were ranked by the number of aligned homologues and all candidate duplications with a <i>P</i>-value &lt;0.001 were retained.</p><p>Synonymous Ks and non-synonymous Ka substitution rates for duplicated genes were determined with Smith–Waterman alignments of protein sequences and subsequently derived codon-based alignments<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Rice, P., Longden, I. &amp; Bleasby, A. EMBOSS: the European Molecular Biology Open Software Suite. Trends Genet. 16, 276–277 (2000)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d59549e3417">62</a></sup>. Rates were computed by the Nei–Gojobori method as implemented in the KaKsCalculator tool<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Zhang, Z. et al. KaKs_Calculator: calculating Ka and Ks through model selection and model averaging. Genomics Proteomics Bioinformatics 4, 259–263 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d59549e3421">63</a></sup>. Previous studies had shown a strong dependency of Ks values on the GC3 composition of gene pairs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Tang, H., Bowers, J. E., Wang, X. &amp; Paterson, A. H. Angiosperm genome comparisons reveal early polyploidy in the monocot lineage. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 107, 472–477 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d59549e3425">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Shi, X. et al. Nucleotide substitution pattern in rice paralogues: implication for negative correlation between the synonymous substitution rate and codon usage bias. Gene 376, 199–206 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d59549e3428">64</a></sup>. We therefore analysed Ks values of gene pairs separately for GC3-high (GC3&gt;75% for both genes), -medium (exactly one gene with GC3&gt;75%) and -low (GC3≤75% for both genes) pairs. Divergence time estimates were based on histogram peak Ks values of the low pairs and a molecular clock of <i>λ</i>=6.5 × 10–9 synonymous substitutions per site and year<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Gaut, B. S., Morton, B. R., McCaig, B. C. &amp; Clegg, M. T. Substitution rate comparisons between grasses and palms: synonymous rate differences at the nuclear gene Adh parallel rate differences at the plastid gene rbcL. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 93, 10274–10279 (1996)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d59549e3435">65</a></sup>. Divergence times <i>T</i> were computed as <i>T</i>=2<i>λ</i>Ks. We emphasize, however, that all estimates might be biased by the unusual GC3 content of Spirodela genes and possible rate differences that had been reported in monocots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Gaut, B. S., Morton, B. R., McCaig, B. C. &amp; Clegg, M. T. Substitution rate comparisons between grasses and palms: synonymous rate differences at the nuclear gene Adh parallel rate differences at the plastid gene rbcL. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 93, 10274–10279 (1996)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d59549e3449">65</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec29">Gene family list</h3><p>To extend the strict gene family list and include closely related candidate in-paralogues from distinct clusters, we determined for each cluster the minimal intra-cluster similarity/threshold T of its members using an all-against-all blastp comparison between all genomes. The minimal intra-cluster threshold Ti was defined as the minimal expectation value <i>E</i> of all pairwise similarity comparisons between members of cluster i. In addition, it was restricted to a maximal value of <i>E</i>≤10–30. Next, we expanded our strict gene family list by including all matches that exceeded threshold Ti to any member of the ith cluster and derived by this procedure the extended gene family list. For both gene lists, multiple protein sequence alignments were computed using MUSCLE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Edgar, R. C. MUSCLE: a multiple sequence alignment method with reduced time and space complexity. BMC Bioinformatics 5, 113 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d59549e3467">48</a></sup>. Alignments were checked by Gblocks<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Castresana, J. Selection of conserved blocks from multiple alignments for their use in phylogenetic analysis. Mol. Biol. Evol. 17, 540–552 (2000)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d59549e3471">66</a></sup> and manual curation. Phylogenetic trees were constructed using FastTree<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Price, M. N., Dehal, P. S. &amp; Arkin, A. P. FastTree 2—approximately maximum-likelihood trees for large alignments. PLoS One 5, e9490 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d59549e3475">67</a></sup>. Visualization and analysis of phylogenetic trees was performed with custom-made python scripts, the python module ETE2 and iHOP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Huerta-Cepas, J., Dopazo, J. &amp; Gabaldon, T. ETE: a python Environment for Tree Exploration. BMC Bioinformatics 11, 24 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d59549e3480">68</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Letunic, I. &amp; Bork, P. Interactive Tree Of Life (iTOL): an online tool for phylogenetic tree display and annotation. Bioinformatics 23, 127–128 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms4311#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d59549e3483">69</a></sup>. Trees were manually inspected for reduced and amplified copy numbers of orthologous genes between Spirodela and other plant species and results were analysed by searches of the known literature.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec30" data-title="Additional information"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec30-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec30">Additional information</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec30-content"><p><b>Accession numbers:</b> The genome sequence of <i>Spirodela polyrhiza</i> strain 7498 has been deposited in DDBJ/EMBL/GenBank nucleotide core database under accession code <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=ATDW00000000">ATDW00000000</a>. BAC End sequences have been deposited in the GenBank GSS database under accession codes <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=JY978532">JY978532</a> to <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=KG007076">KG007076</a>. Fosmid sequences have been deposited in the GenBank nucleotide core database under accession codes <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=AC254537">AC254537</a> to <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=AC254559">AC254559</a></p><p><b>How to cite this article:</b> Wang, W. <i>et al.</i> The <i>Spirodela polyrhiza</i> genome reveals insights into its neotenous reduction fast growth and aquatic lifestyle. <i>Nat. Commun.</i> 5:3311 doi: 10.1038/ncomms4311 (2014).</p></div></div></section>
